        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c03971"><meta property="og:title" content="Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/acsami.4c03971.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="An efficient interface and composition modification strategy is proposed to significantly increase the rate capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of SnO anodes via energy band structure modulation for Na-ion batteries (SIBs). The as-fabricated SnO@Bi@C material with Bi nanocrystals homogeneously dispersed on and around the SnO surface is prepared by an electrospinning method. The excellently dispersed Bi nanocrystals realize an effective interface contact with SnO and Na2O, which effectively lowers the electron conduction barriers and promotes sodium-ion release and transport upon the desodiation process, increasing the rate capacity and ICE of the SnO anode. The prepared SnO@Bi@C exhibits a much higher rate capacity up to 10 A g–1, except an improved ICE of 80.67%, compared to 57.38% of SnO@C. Importantly, the intercalation of Na+ between layer-structured Bi galleries provides a good basis for excellent cycle stability due to the highways constructed inside the prepared composites. This finding provides an effective tactic to establish ion transport high-speed channels, which essentially increase the rate performance and the ICE of the SnO-based anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/acsami.4c03971.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93188421c81154b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188421c81154b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '106afabc-4288-4cc9-b94e-b2d48b7e0d9b'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188421c81154b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="93188421c81154b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Energy Band-Modulated SnO Anodes with Improved Rate Capacity and Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c03971", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:xuliding@just.edu.cn">xuliding@just.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Xuli++Ding" data-id="article_author_search">Xuli Ding</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" The as-fabricated SnO@Bi@C material with Bi nanocrystals homogeneously dispersed on and around the SnO surface is prepared by an electrospinning method. The excellently dispersed Bi nanocrystals realize an effective interface contact with SnO and Na<sub>2</sub>O, which effectively lowers the electron conduction barriers and promotes sodium-ion release and transport upon the desodiation process, increasing the rate capacity and ICE of the SnO anode. The prepared SnO@Bi@C exhibits a much higher rate capacity up to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, except an improved ICE of 80.67%, compared to 57.38% of SnO@C. Importantly, the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> between layer-structured Bi galleries provides a good basis for excellent cycle stability due to the highways constructed inside the prepared composites. This finding provides an effective tactic to establish ion transport high-speed channels, which essentially increase the rate performance and the ICE of the SnO-based anodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D39206%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWang%2BYao%252C%2BQiang%2BLi%252C%2BPeng%2BQin%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D30%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c03971%26title%3DEnergy%2BBand-Modulated%2BSnO%2BAnodes%2Bwith%2BImproved%2BRate%2BCapacity%2Band%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D39214%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290817&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Interfaces"> Interfaces </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bi+Nanocrystal&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Bi%20nanocrystal">Bi nanocrystal</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sno+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SnO%20anode">SnO anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interface+Contact&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interface%20contact">interface contact</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_rate%20capability">rate capability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The fossil energy crisis and environmental pollution issues have promoted the vigorous blossom of electric vehicles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> As the core component, high specific energy secondary ion batteries with high power density have been eagerly concerned. However, compared with quick refueling, the long charging time of electric vehicles limits their time efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> Therefore, it is critical to tackle the fast charging issues faced by the battery high-tech industries. Particularly, it is deliberated that fast charging is basically achieved by two ways: one is high-voltage charging, and the other is high-current charging. However, the high-voltage charging must be achieved under the premise of improving the vehicle insulation level and the strength of the electronic components, which will greatly increase the production cost of electric vehicles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Hence, high-current charging is the main form of fast charging at present, which mainly depends on the electrode materials, electrolytes, and charge transfer interfaces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> At present, microdesign of the anode itself plays a decisive role in the fast-charging performance. For anode materials, the idea of fast-charging design is attributed as follows: First of all, it improves the ionic and electronic conductivity of the material and accelerates ion and charge transfer. Besides, it enhances the infiltration ability of the electrolyte on the surface of the negative electrode material and reduces the interface transfer resistance. Furthermore, it inhibits the structural transformation and volume expansion of the material under high-current conditions and improves the cycle life. Of the promising electrode candidates, SnO is regarded as a potential anode material for high specific energy sodium-ion batteries due to its high theoretical specific capacity (1150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), abundant reserves, nontoxicity, and low cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> However, due to the poor conductivity and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, the electrochemical performance of SnO is still seriously restrained. By contrast, bismuth (Bi) is recently reported as fast-charging anode material for SIBs due to its layer structure, but it is still frustrated by the limited specific capacity of 385 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> Besides, due to the anisotropy of the layer structure, when Na<sup>+</sup> ions are inserted into the Bi interlayers, they enter from the edge of the layered area and have a long diffusion path, resulting in the rate performance being severely suppressed. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare the nanosized Bi particles; specifically, the channels for Na<sup>+</sup> ion migration should be shorter, which is more conducive to the movement of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the lifting of fast-charging capability. In consideration of both merits and deficiency between SnO and Bi, in this work, we design and fabricate SnO@Bi@C nanofibers, in which SnO and Bi nanoparticles were well coated by porous carbon nanofibers. Correspondingly, the prepared SnO@Bi@C composite has the following virtues: First, the introduced Bi nanoparticles around the SnO build an ion transfer bridge between SnO particles, which strengthens the connection between SnO particles and improves the ion transport and shift kinetics through SnO anodes. Second, the sodiation/desodiation reaction reversibility of SnO can be improved due to the presence of conducting-phase Bi and the good interface contact with the energy band downward, which is a superhighway for Na<sup>+</sup>. Third, the volume expansion of SnO is partly buffered due to the lower volume change of Bi during the charging process. Finally, the porous carbon nanofibers not only provide more active sites for the Na<sup>+</sup> storage but also raise the mechanical strength for the fabricated electrode. Accordingly, when used as anodes, the fabricated SnO@Bi@C composite can deliver 529 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> reversible capacity and 83.2% initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), reaching a long cycle life of 4000 cycles at the current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This work provides a new strategy for the preparation of low-cost oxides for a high-rate and long-cycle sodium-ion battery. Especially, the embedding of Bi nano species around SnO particles may be a viable approach to obtain fast-charging SIBs with oxides as anodes.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.  Preparation of SnO@Bi@PCNF Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">BiCl<sub>3</sub> (Aladdin, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 315.34, 99%), commercial SnO (Aladdin, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 134.71, 99%), polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 1,300,000, Aladdin), and methyl methacrylate (PMMA, Aladdin) were used as raw materials. The SnO particles were first ball milled for 10 h by high-energy ball milling. 1 mmol BiCl<sub>3</sub> was put into 10 mL of <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF), and 0.6 g of PVP with 0.6 g of PMMA was added, stirring continuously overnight. Then, the suspension with 1 mmol of SnO was ultra sounded for 10 min before the solution was put into two 10 mL syringes to carry out the electrostatic spinning. The positive and negative voltages were set to +17 and −3 kV, respectively, and the injection speed of the syringe was set to 0.08 mm/min. After that, the nanofibers were collected with aluminum foil and dried overnight in a 50 °C oven. The dried fibers were placed in a tubular furnace and heated to 600 °C in an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> mixture for 2 h. Finally, the samples were denoted as SnO@Bi@C-1, SnO@Bi@C-2, and SnO@Bi@C-3 according to SnO and BiCl<sub>3</sub> different molar ratios of 1:0.5, 1:1, and 1:2, respectively. In addition, for comparison, the SnO@C sample was also prepared.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The internal microstructure and phase composition of the SnO@Bi@C samples were analyzed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) using a JEOL JEM-2100 CCD camera. The crystal structure evolution and phase structure evolution were studied by an X’Pert PRO diffractometer (XRD, Shimadzu, CuKα irradiation, λ = 1.5406 Å, scanning rate 10 min<sup>–1</sup>). In addition, the elemental valence state of the samples was also analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ESCALAB 250Xi). In addition, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) tests were performed in air with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and in the temperature range of 25 to 800 °C using a zero-friction vertical dilatometer (L75VS, LINSEIS, Germany).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The slurry was composed of 70 wt % active substance (SnO@Bi@C), 15 wt % carbon black (Super P), and 15 wt % sodium alginate as the binder. The working electrode was assembled into a CR2032 button cell battery in a glovebox filled with argon (O<sub>2</sub> &lt;1.0 ppm, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt;1.0 ppm). In addition, the full battery was also assembled using Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) as cathode. The electrolyte consisted of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in dimethyl ether (DME, 100%). A glass fiber (Whatman GF/D) and sodium foil were selected for the diaphragm and counter electrode, respectively. The assembled battery was placed on hold for 8 h before testing. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed using a DH7001 electrochemical workstation with frequencies and scan rates, respectively, ranging from 1 MHz to 0.1 Hz and 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. All batteries were cycled on Landt (Wuhan) and Neware (Shenzhen) multichannel battery test systems with discharge cutoff voltage 0.005 V and charge cutoff voltage 1.5 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). All battery-specific capacity calculations were based on the weight of the active substance in the electrode.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a> shows the schematic diagram of the preparation process for the SnO@Bi@C composite. As shown, the SnO and Bi nanoparticles are encapsulated in porous carbon fibers by the electrospinning method and thermal treat process for carbonization. The unique structure is mainly manifested in two aspects. One is that the pores in the carbon fiber can provide more active sites for ion transfer and electrolyte permeation, except that the fiber structure has strong mechanical strength and sufficient space. For the other, it should be noted that the conducting-phase Bi is a layered structure, which provides fast diffusion channels for sodium-ion transport. This special design can not only improve the reversible capacity of SnO anodes but also ensure long cycle life and high-rate capability.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the preparation process for the SnO@Bi@C composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TEM images for the prepared SnO@Bi@C-2 composite are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>. It is observed that a large number of nanoparticles with sizes of 20–80 nm are dispersed inside the fibers from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a–c, and the widths of the fibers are mainly in the range of 200–500 nm. In addition, pores with 10–50 nm are also found on the surface and edge of fibers, which is generated due to the PMMA evaporating in the carbonization process. Meanwhile, the parallel and ordered lattice stripes are clearly visible in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images, indicating a better crystal structure of the prepared samples. Moreover, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, the lattice spacings of 0.327, 0.237, and 0.227 nm, respectively, correspond to the planes of (012), (104), and (110) of Bi. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> By contrast, the SnO (112), (020), and (021) planes, respectively, exhibit interlayer spacings of 0.315, 0.286, and 0.278 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> Importantly, it is also found that the different Bi and SnO phases are arranged at alternate intervals, which is conducive to accelerate ion transport and release stress generated during sodiation. The energy dispersive spectrum (EDS) in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f–h further indicates the uniform distribution of the Bi, Sn, O, and C elements in the composite.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a–c) Low-resolution TEM images of the SnO@Bi@C fibers at different test regions. (d, e) HRTEM images with parallel fringes of Bi and SnO. (f–h) Mapping image for the observed region and corresponding elements mapping of C, O, Sn, and Bi.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The inner phase and structure evolution for different samples are characterized by the XRD patterns and shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a; as indicated, the strongest diffraction peaks at ∼27.2o and ∼28.6o are assigned to the characteristic peaks of Bi and SnO, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 13 and 15 to 17">(13,15−17)</a> respectively, suggesting that the pure Bi phase is obtained in the prepared samples. In order to further explore the element valence change in the fabricated composite, XPS was also used to reveal the inner information. As indicated by the full-spectrum XPS in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, the elements of Sn, Bi, and C are all included and the valence states evolving in each element are distinguished further in the high-resolution XPS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c–f). As seen, in the high-resolution Bi 4f (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), it is divided into four bands: two bands at ∼156.8 and ∼162.1 eV are ascribed to the zero-valence Bi (Bi0), and the others at ∼158.8 and ∼164.1 eV are attributed to trivalent Bi (Bi3+). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> The occurrence of Bi3+ may be due to the oxidation reaction on the surface of Bi nanoparticles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Similarly, in the high-resolution Sn 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), the peaks at ∼486.7 and ∼494.8 eV correspond to Sn2+ that come from SnO; correspondingly, the peaks at ∼484.8 and ∼493.4 eV belong to zero-valence Sn. The reason for the appearance of Sn0 is because some of SnO is reduced to metal Sn in the process of carbonization. Moreover, in the C 1s high-resolution spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e), 284.8, 286.7, and 288.7 eV belong to the C–C, C–O, and C═O bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Moreover, the corresponding O 1s at 530.4 and 533.1 eV, separately, belong to the O–Sn and O═C bonds. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f, it is found that the content of O–C═O and C═O in SnO@Bi@C-2 is obviously higher than that of SnO@Bi@C-1 and SnO@Bi@C-3, although the exact reason is still under exploration; it is suggested that more O element in the SnO may interact with the C in the fiber to form the O–C═O and C═O bonds in SnO@Bi@C-2 than in SnO@Bi@C-1 and SnO@Bi@C-3. In addition, it is also found that the least amount of Sn–O is in SnO@Bi@C-2 and the most amount is in SnO@Bi@C-3. Therefore, we speculate that the O element in SnO is bonded to the C element in the fibers.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of SnO@Bi@C-1, SnO@Bi@C-2, and SnO@Bi@C-3 samples. (b) XPS spectra of SnO@Bi@C-1, SnO@Bi@C-2, and SnO@Bi@C-3 samples. High-resolution XPS maps of (c) Bi 4f, (d) Sn 3d, (e) C 1s, and (f) Sn 3d.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the fabricated anodes is further evaluated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>. As plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, the profiles of the first discharge/charge of SnO@C and SnO@Bi@C-2 anodes cycled at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were compared. It is obvious that the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode exhibits a much higher ICE of 80.67%, which is a significant increase compared to 57.38% for the SnO@C anode. Such a high ICE increment ∼23.29% is the largest one ever reported (seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g), except for the presodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> In addition, the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode also shows a relatively high sodiation voltage compared to the SnO@C anode, especially in the first discharge process shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, suggesting the fast sodiation reaction kinetics. Particularly, in the desodiation process, different samples display similar voltage terraces at ∼0.60 and ∼0.77 V, demonstrating the low polarization in the fabricated anodes. More information about the ICE compared for various samples is summarized in supporting <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S1 and Table S1</a>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties of different anode materials in sodium-ion half cells. (a) Comparison of ICE from the first charge and discharge plots at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) EIS plots of different samples. (c) CV plotting for sample SnO@Bi@C-2 in the original three cycles. (d) Rate property comparison of different samples as current densities changed from 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, to 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (e) Cycle performance and capacity retention of sample SnO@Bi@C-2 in the first 300 cycles at currents of 2.0 and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Long cycle of 4000 cycles of SnO@Bi@C-2 at a current density of 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (g) Comparison of the ICE of different samples previously reported.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to further explore the charge transport kinetics, EIS tests were carried out and impedance spectral fitting was assessed based on the testing results. As indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, the EIS plotting is constituted of a semicircle in the high-frequency region and a slant line in the low-frequency region. Definitely, the diameter of the semicircle represents the charge transfer impedance at the interface and the larger diameter indicates greater charge transfer resistance, while the inclined line reflects the diffusion impedance of Na<sup>+</sup> and the larger slope of the slash indicates smaller diffusion resistance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 23 to 27">(23−27)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b is the equivalent circuit where <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> represents the solution impedance, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> represents the charge transfer impedance, <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> represents the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion impedance, and CPE<sub>ct</sub> represents the capacitance associated with charge transfer. Importantly, according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 23 to 27">(23−27)</a> different points were selected in the region of 0.1–1.0 Hz and used to fit the linear relationship between the real part impedance <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and the frequency ω<sup>–1/2</sup>, which is also plotted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>.<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-5"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-6" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">Z</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-7" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">re</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-8"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-9" style="margin-left: 0.333em; As seen from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode has the smallest slope value (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub> = 0.45) while the SnO@C anode has the largest slope value (<i>k</i><sub>4</sub> = 0.73); the other samples have <i>k</i> values in 0.45–0.73. To further obtain the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion character among the compared samples, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> was used to further calculate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 23 to 27">(23−27)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display"
