id="MJXp-Span-41" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-43"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">A</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-45" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-46"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-47" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">n</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-48" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">4</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-49"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-50" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">F</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-51" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">4</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-52"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-53" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">C</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-54" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">2</span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-55"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-56" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">k</span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-57" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>D</mi><mrow><mi>Na</mi><mo>+</mo></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>0.5</mn><msup><mi>R</mi><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mi>T</mi><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>/</mo><msup><mi>A</mi><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mi>n</mi><mn>4</mn></msup><msup><mi>F</mi><mn>4</mn></msup><msup><mi>C</mi><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where <i>R</i> represents the gas constant (8.314 J mol<sup>–1</sup> K<sup>–1</sup>), <i>T</i> represents the temperature, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred per molar, <i>A</i> is the reaction area of the electrode (cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>F</i> is Faraday’s constant (96,485 C mol<sup>–1</sup>), and <i>C</i> represents the concentration of Na+ ions. Correspondingly, the diffusion coefficients are 2.83 × 10<sup>–6</sup> and 1.07 × 10<sup>–7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, for the SnO@Bi@C-2 and SnO@C anodes, which suggests that the introduction of Bi nanocrystals does enhance the ion diffusion capability in the fabricated SnO@Bi@C anodes. Furthermore, in order to deeply comprehend the Na+ storage kinetics, the CV testing for the first three cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> was performed in the voltage range 0.005–2.0 V (vs Na/Na+) for different samples. As compared in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, a distinct difference is observed between the SnO@C and SnO@Bi@C anodes where CV curves show less symmetry in the former but better in the latter. In addition, the initial cycles in the SnO@Bi@C anodes also overlap well. The symmetry character and good overlapping are two important indications to corroborate the good reversibility in the SnO@Bi@C anodes; the more symmetric and better the overlap, the better the reversibility. In the initial three discharge/charge cycles, the first wide cathode band extended in the range 0.70–0.20 V is obviously different from the others, which is attributed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI). The cathodic peaks at ∼0.67, ∼0.45, and ∼0.04 eV, respectively, are attributed to the gradual alloying of Bi to form NaBi and Na3 Bi, as well as Sn to NaxSn. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 16, 17, 28 and 29">(16,17,28,29)</a> By contrast, in the subsequent anodic scanning, two sharp oxidation peaks at 0.62 and 0.75 V are ascribed to the dealloying of Na3 Bi to NaBi and NaBi to Bi, respectively. Similarly, the weak peak at 0.08 V is due to the dealloying of the NaxSn phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 16, 17, 28 and 29">(16,17,28,29)</a> In addition, it should be pointed out that the reduction peak at 0.04 V and the oxidation peak at 0.08 V, which come from the alloy and dealloy of Sn, respectively, appeared in the SnO@Bi@C anode but were not observed in the SnO@C anode, indicating that the presence of Bi facilitates more NaxSn phase generation in the SnO anode, which is beneficial to improve the ICE and reversible Na+ ion storage. Particularly, under the same voltage range, the redox peaks of the SnO@Bi@C anodes become very obvious, suggesting that the addition of the Bi element does improve the reaction kinetics, leading to the elevated reversibility and improved ICE. Furthermore, the rate performance is also shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. As the current densities increased from 0.05 to 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode delivers capacities of 342, 320, 315, 310, 300, 290, 275, and 252 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. When the current switches on 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> again, the specific capacity recovers to the initial capacity again, meaning the good reversibility of the fabricated electrode. Differently, the SnO@C electrode displays inferior rate capability, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d; when the current is increased, the electrode capacitance decays rapidly, and as the current is increased to 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode capacity attenuates almost to zero. In parallel, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e shows the cycle performance comparison in the initial 300 cycles between current 2.0 and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode. As shown, in the first several cycles, the specific capacity undergoes rapid decay, but after that, it can remain stable in the following hundreds or even thousands of cycles. After 300 cycles, the capacity retention can reach 94.4 and 88.1%, respectively, at current 2.0 and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Similarly, in the subsequent long cycle up to 4000 cycles at a current density of 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity converts from the initially stable 290 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> into the ultimate 182 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting a capacity loss of 0.008% for each cycle. The present work was also compared with the reported strategies in terms of electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), which shows that the present work exhibits a competitive advantage in the compared samples.</div><div class="NLM_p">As further clarified in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>, the different Na<sup>+</sup> transport mechanisms are suggested between the SnO@C and SnO@Bi@C anodes during the charging process. For the SnO@C anode, the SnO phase converted to Sn and Na<sub>2</sub>O phases during the first discharge and the intermediate tetrahedral structure Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn phase is formed during the further alloying process. Due to the tetrahedral clusters greatly hindering the embedding and removal of Na<sup>+</sup>, the alloying and dealloying reactions of SnO become difficult. On the contrary, in the SnO@Bi@C anode, the intermediate phases NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi formed during the first alloying process belong to the layered structure; especially, Na<sub>3</sub>Bi is a high-speed ion conductor. This unique layered structure plays a critical role in promoting Na<sup>+</sup> transfer in the fabricated electrodes. Particularly, it is equivalent to establishing fast channels for the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and shift between the isolated SnO particles and thus improving the reaction kinetic of Na<sup>+</sup> in the SnO-based anodes.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Different Na<sup>+</sup> transport mechanisms between the SnO@C and SnO@Bi@C anodes during the cycle process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to gain an in-depth view into the electrochemical reaction mechanism of Na+ with the prepared samples, ex-situ XRD was also used to investigate the fabricated anodes at different charging and discharging levels in the first cycle. As revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>, when the SnO@C anode was discharged to 0.04 V, the XRD pattern shows that SnO has been almost reduced and new phases Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn can be detected, which is ascribed to the conversion of SnO to Sn and Na<sub>2</sub>O and the alloying between Na<sup>+</sup> and Sn, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref30" aria-label="reference 13 and 30">(13,30)</a> while in the charging to 0.23 V, the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn (<i>x</i> &lt; 3.75) phase appears due to the dealloying of Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> In the whole discharging and charging cycles, Na<sub>2</sub>O always exists, indicating the irreversibility of the reaction. In contrast, for the SnO@Bi@C-2 anode, the biggest difference compared to the SnO@C anode is that the concentration of the Na<sub>2</sub>O phase is significantly reduced in the roundtrip cycle. The result indicates that the presence of the Bi element helps to reduce the irreversible Na<sub>2</sub>O phase; the concrete reason will be discussed in detail later. Additionally, it should be noted that some peaks are shifted with the progress of the reaction, as revealed in the enlarged regions in the XRD patterns in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. It is found, in the first discharge process to 0.45 V, that the NaBi phase that posited between 50 and 55° shifted significantly to a small diffraction angle direction. We speculate that it is caused by the expanded lattice spacing when the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were transported in the layer structure of NaBi. Meanwhile, the Sn phase shifted in the vicinity of 45°, as it was discharged to 0.04 V, which is attributed to the formation of different Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn phases with different Na contents in the sodiation process.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XRD patterns after discharging and charging at different voltage levels: (a) SnO@C anode and (b) SnO@Bi@C-2 anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As has been noted, owing to the unique structure in SnO@Bi@C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>, it is suggested that the Bi matrix can form a stable layer backbone to build Na<sup>+</sup> ion high-speed transport channels, leading to an excellent rate capability and high ICE. Inversely, SnO is a tetrahedron structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a), which hinders the ion migration, but the layered Bi can provide more channels as highways for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer. Predominately, with regard to the contact interface between SnO and Bi, the contact feature between both of them plays an important role in electron transfer across the interface, which is mainly determined by the work function of the two considered solids (W<sub>s</sub>:SnO, W<sub>B</sub>:Bi). As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, the work functions of SnO (W<sub>s</sub>) and Na<sub>2</sub>O are ∼4.4 and ∼4.56 eV, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> respectively, and ∼4.2 eV for Bi (W<sub>B</sub>); thus, the free electrons in Bi mitigate smoothly to the surface of SnO and Na<sub>2</sub>O owing to the Fermi level difference in height, forming a region where the energy band is bent downward. This energy level adjustment causes the SnO/Na<sub>2</sub>O surface to form a highly conductive region because electrons are more easily moved in this region. Subsequently, in the desodiation process, the electrons can easily be released from the SnO and Na<sub>2</sub>O to surface Bi without a barrier, thus enabling more Na<sup>+</sup> shift and a higher ICE as well as improved rate capability of the SnO@Bi@C electrode. By contrast, the work function of carbon is ∼5.0 eV, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> which is much higher than that of SnO and Na<sub>2</sub>O, and a blocking barrier is formed that impedes the electron transport, resulting in the suppressing release of Na<sup>+</sup> and causing low ICE and poor rate of the SnO@C electrode.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Schematic diagram of ion transport in the layered Bi nanocrystals and electron shift at the interface. (b) Illustration of the interface contact and electron energy barrier forming between materials with different work functions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the good electrochemical performance of the fabricated electrode, the full cell coupled with the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> cathode was assembled. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a shows the work principle schematic diagram, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b shows the charge and discharge curves of the full battery in the voltage window of 2.0–4.0 V. The rate performance for the fabricated full battery is also demonstrated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c), as the current density changed increasing from 30, 50, 100, to 150 and 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, the full battery can deliver specific capacities of 101, 52, 28, 25, and 20 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. When the current density is restored to 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity reaches 55 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> again, indicating that the fabricated sodium-ion full cell can bear the impact of high current density and exhibit good structural stability. Meanwhile, the cyclic performance test of the full battery was also carried out; as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d, after 65 cycles at a testing current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the full battery can still provide a specific capacity of 51.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In addition, the LED lights are successfully lit as also shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d, suggesting practical application on the full battery. The results further indicate that the fabricated SnO@Bi@C electrode has a significant potential as the anode for the next-generation high specific energy sodium-ion batteries. More information about the cycle property and charge and discharge character of the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> cathode is also feasible in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/medium/am4c03971_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Schematic diagram of the working principle of the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>||SnO@Bi@C full cell. (b) Charge and discharge properties of the full cell. (c) Rate performance and (d) cycling performance at 50 mAg<sup>–1</sup> for the fabricated full battery.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/asset/images/large/am4c03971_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c03971&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a good interface contact between SnO and Bi was constructed through the composite SnO@Bi@C fabricated by a simple electrospinning method. With the special layer structure of Bi nanoparticles around SnO particles, the ion highways are built inner to facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Most importantly, the proper work function match between SnO, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and Bi cut down the energy barrier of electron transfer between different contact interfaces, subsequently promoting the Na<sup>+</sup> ion release from the Na<sub>2</sub>O phase. The good interface contact combined with high-speed ion channels; the fabricated SnO@Bi@C exhibits a high ICE of 80.62%, compared to 57.38% of SnO@C. In addition, the excellent rate up to 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> was also achieved in the fabricated anodes. The reversible capacity reached 529 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and the capacity retention rates remained at 94.4 and 88.1% after 300 cycles at different current densities of 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 4000 cycles under the condition of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity can still reach 182 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating good cycle stability. It is worth noting that the full battery assembled from the Na(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> cathode and SnO@Bi@C-2 anode can still provide a reversible capacity of 51.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. This work provides an effective strategy to conquer the sluggish ion transfer kinetics of oxide-based anodes for the next-generation sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c03971?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c03971</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label"><li><p class="inline">Electrochemical performance and summary comparison of the reported related works (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx" class="ext-link">DOCX</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c03971/suppl_file/am4c03971_si_001.docx">am4c03971_si_001.docx (4.04 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c03971" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Therefore, significant improvements to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in terms of energy d. and cost along the battery value chain are required, while other key performance indicators, such as lifetime, safety, fast-charging ability and low-temp. performance, need to be enhanced or at least sustained.  Here, we review advances and challenges in LIB materials for automotive applications, in particular with respect to cost and performance parameters.  The prodn. processes of anode and cathode materials are discussed, focusing on material abundance and cost.  Advantages and challenges of different types of electrolyte for automotive batteries are examd.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review on energy chemistry of fast-charging anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cai, Wenlong; Yao, Yu-Xing; Zhu, Gao-Long; Yan, Chong; Jiang, Li-Li; He, Chuanxin; Huang, Jia-Qi; Zhang, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the impetus to accelerate worldwide market adoption of elec. vehicles and afford consumer electronics with better user experience, advancing fast-charging technol. is an inevitable trend.  However, current high-energy lithium-ion batteries are unable to support ultrafast power input without any adverse consequences, with the capacity fade and safety concerns of the mainstream graphite-based anodes being the key technol. barrier.  The aim of this review is to summarise the fundamentals, challenges, and solns. to enable graphite anodes that are capable of high-rate charging.  First, we explore the complicated yet intriguing graphite-electrolyte interface during intercalation based on existing theories.  Second, we analyze the key dilemmas facing fast-charging graphite anodes.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy storage will be key to overcoming the intermittency and variability of renewable energy sources.  Here, we propose a metric for the cost of energy storage and for identifying optimally sized storage systems.  The levelized cost of energy storage is the minimum price per kWh that a potential investor requires in order to break even over the entire lifetime of the storage facility.  We forecast the dynamics of this cost metric in the context of lithium-ion batteries and demonstrate its usefulness in identifying an optimally sized battery charged by an incumbent solar PV system.  Applying the model to residential solar customers in Germany, we find that behind-the-meter storage is economically viable because of the large difference between retail rates and current feed-in tariffs.  In contrast, investment incentives for battery systems in California derive principally from a state-level subsidy program.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium and Sodium-Ion Batteries: 50 Years of Research</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prospects in anode materials for sodium ion batteries - A review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Perveen, Tahira; Siddiq, Muhammad; Shahzad, Nadia; Ihsan, Rida; Ahmad, Abrar; Shahzad, Muhammad Imran</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Renewable &amp; Sustainable Energy Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the rapid expansion in energy demands and depletion of fossil fuel reservoirs, the importance of clean energy prodn. and storage has increased drastically.  The renewable energy recourses are cost effective, sustainable and carbon dioxide emission free alternatives.  Nevertheless, this energy is not always available and needs to be stored.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are rapidly used in various applications such as powering electronics, elec. vehicles and grid energy storage.  However, the increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy and rise in cost of LIBs due to limited availability of lithium reserves arises doubts whether LIBs alone can meet the rising demands for mid-to-large-scale energy storage.  Therefore, attention has been shifted towards development of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) which have wide reserves and low precursor cost and thus is considered as appropriate choice for solar and wind energy development.  The prime problem encountered in development of large-scale SIBs is the low effectiveness of appropriate anode material because of large size and sluggish kinetics of Na ions.  A comprehensive study regarding anode materials is reported focusing on storage mechanism and structural changes involved during storage of Na ions in various classes of anode materials including carbon-based materials, conversion, conversion/alloying and org. materials.  A brief overview of various components of SIBs such as cathode, electrolyte and separator are also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.3c02892"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advanced Anode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qiao, Shuangyan; Zhou, Qianwen; Ma, Meng; Liu, Hua Kun; Dou, Shi Xue; Chong, Shaokun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as promising energy storage devices owing to the similar "rocking chair" working mechanism as lithium-ion batteries and abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  However, the large ionic radius of the Na-ion (1.07 Å) brings a key scientific challenge, restricting the development of electrode materials for SIBs, and the infeasibility of graphite and silicon in reversible Na-ion storage further promotes the investigation of advanced anode materials.  Currently, the key issues facing anode materials include sluggish electrochem. kinetics and a large vol. expansion.  Despite these challenges, substantial conceptual and exptl. progress has been made in the past.  Herein, we present a brief review of the recent development of intercalation, conversion, alloying, conversion-alloying, and org. anode materials for SIBs.  Starting from the historical research progress of anode electrodes, the detailed Na-ion storage mechanism is analyzed.  Various optimization strategies to improve the electrochem. properties of anodes are summarized, including phase state adjustment, defect introduction, mol. engineering, nanostructure design, composite construction, heterostructure synthesis, and heteroatom doping.  Furthermore, the assocd. merits and drawbacks of each class of material are outlined, and the challenges and possible future directions for high-performance anode materials are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b05280"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schwingenschlögl, U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alshareef, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We have systematically changed the no. of at. layers stacked in 2D SnO nanosheet anodes and studied their sodium ion battery (SIB) performance.  The results indicate that as the no. of at. SnO layers in a sheet decreases, both the capacity and cycling stability of the Na ion battery improve.  The thinnest SnO nanosheet anodes (two to six SnO monolayers) exhibited the best performance.  Specifically, an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1072 and 848 mAh g-1 were obsd., resp., at 0.1 A g-1.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling 100C Fast-Charging Bulk Bi Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Young-Hoon; An, Jae-Hyun; Kim, Sung-Yeob; Li, Xiangmei; Song, Eun-Ji; Park, Jae-Ho; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Choi, Yong-Seok; Scanlon, David O.; Ahn, Hyo-Jun; Lee, Jae-Chul</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is challenging to develop alloying anodes with ultrafast charging and large energy storage using bulk anode materials because of the difficulty of carrier-ion diffusion and fragmentation of the active electrode material.  Herein, a rational strategy is reported to design bulk Bi anodes for Na-ion batteries that feature ultrafast charging, long cyclability, and large energy storage without using expensive nanomaterials and surface modifications.  It is found that bulk Bi particles gradually transform into a porous nanostructure during cycling in a glyme-based electrolyte, whereas the resultant structure stores Na ions by forming phases with high Na diffusivity.  These features allow the anodes to exhibit unprecedented electrochem. properties; the developed Na-Bi half-cell delivers 379 mA h g-1 (97% of that measured at 1C) at 7.7 A g-1 (20C) during 3500 cycles.  It also retained 94% and 93% of the capacity measured at 1C even at extremely fast-charging rates of 80C and 100C, resp.  The structural origins of the measured properties are verified by expts. and first-principles calcns.  The findings of this study not only broaden understanding of the underlying mechanisms of fast-charging anodes, but also provide basic guidelines for searching battery anodes that simultaneously exhibit high capacities, fast kinetics, and long cycling stabilities.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202202673"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Integrating Bi@C Nanospheres in Porous Hard Carbon Frameworks for Ultrafast Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Integrating Bi@C Nanospheres in Porous Hard Carbon Frameworks for Ultrafast Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Yazhan; Song, Ning; Zhang, Zhengchunyu; Chen, Weihua; Feng, Jinkui; Xi, Baojuan; Xiong, Shenglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as an alternative technol. because of their merits in abundance and cost.  Realizing their real applications, however, remains a formidable challenge.  One is that among the limitations of anode materials, the alloy-type candidates tolerate fast capacity fading during cycling.  Here, a 3D framework superstructure assembled with carbon nanobelt arrays decorated with a metallic bismuth (Bi) nanospheres coated carbon layer by thermolysis of Bi-based metal-org. framework nanorods is synthesized as an anode material for SIBs.  Due to the unique structural superiority, the anode design promotes excellent sodium-storage performance in terms of high capacity, excellent cycling stability, and ultrahigh rate capability up to 80 A g-1 with a capacity of 308.8 mAh g-1.  The unprecedented sodium-storage ability is not only attributed to the unique hybrid architecture, but also to the prodn. of a homogeneous and thin solid electrolyte interface layer and the formation of uniform porous nanostructures during cycling in the ether-based electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Because of concerns regarding shortages of lithium resources and the urgent need to develop low-cost and high-efficiency energy-storage systems, research and applications of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have re-emerged in recent years.  Herein, recent advances in high-capacity Sn-based anode materials for stable SIBs are highlighted, including tin (Sn) alloys, Sn oxides, Sn sulfides, Sn selenides, Sn phosphides, and their composites.  The reaction mechanisms between Sn-based materials and sodium are clarified.  Multiphase and multiscale structural optimizations of Sn-based materials to achieve good sodium-storage performance are emphasized.  Full-cell designs using Sn-based materials as anodes and further development of Sn-based materials are discussed from a commercialization perspective. Herein, we demonstrate a novel Sn/Sb@SSO@PCFs composite using simple but effective electrospinning methods, which enable SSO nanoparticles embedded with Sn and Sb nanodomains in situ generation.  The SSO was first reported as anode for sodium-ion batteries and in situ generation Sb and Sn nanocrystals domain around the SSO oxides.  Different from existing tactics, the unique composite establishes a robust and efficient elec. contact between the SSO and the conductive Sb and Sn phases, enabling fast ions and electrons transfer across the electrochem. active SSO/Sb/Sn.  Meanwhile, the composites offer an opportunity to compromise the challenges faced by each entity and enhance the overall performance by circumventing the weakness of individual SSO, Sn and Sb.  As evidenced, the as-fabricated composite has unexpected electrochem. properties, including high capacity and long cycle stability with a retention rate of 83% after 450 cycles at 0.5 C.  Combined with synergistic Na+ ions storage of SSO/Sb/Sb and a simple and scalable manufg. process, this study provides a new strategy to modulate material delivering high capacity and cycle stability for the next generation sodium ions batteries (SIBs).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2021.09.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sarkar, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mukherjee, P.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries suffer from low coulombic efficiency, which stems from Na-deficiency and unstable interfaces for both cathode and anode materials.  Exhibiting a favorable solvation structure, Na-naphthalene/tetrahydropyran (Na-NP/THP) is screened and selected as a full-cell presodiation soln. for hard carbon (HC) anodes and a P-2 type Na0.67Fe0.1Al0.1Mn0.8O2 (NFAM) cathode, which are both Na-deficient.  Through a wealth of characterizations, it is revealed that the proposed presodiation agent not only boosts the initial coulombic efficiency of both HC and NFAM electrodes effectively, but also facilitates robust solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) and cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) on both electrodes.  Consequently, the long-term cycling and rate performance are greatly improved thanks to the stabilized structures of electrode materials.  Furthermore, a proof-of-concept NFAM||HC pouch cell based on this presodiation strategy is assembled, showing a high reversible capacity and superior cycling performance.  By offering a remedy to a full cell system subjected to severe Na-deficiency, this work conceptually demonstrates a feasible strategy to fully tap the potential of high-energy-d. materials in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b14381"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moeez, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H. Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Presodiation Strategies and Their Effect on Electrode-Electrolyte Interphases for High-Performance Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                 10.1021/acsami.9b14381</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D41394-41401%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DI.%2BMoeez%26author%3DH.%2BG.%2BJung%26author%3DH.%2BD.%2BLim%26author%3DK.%2BY.%2BChung%26title%3DPresodiation%2BStrategies%2Band%2BTheir%2BEffect%2Bon%2BElectrode-Electrolyte%2BInterphases%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.9b14381&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c03971&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.9b14381&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Presodiation Strategies and Their Effect on Electrode-Electrolyte Interphases for High-Performance Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moeez, Iqra; Jung, Hun-Gi; Lim, Hee-Dae; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">41394-41401</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most active materials for Na-ion batteries suffer from the problem of low-energy efficiency in the 1st cycle because of the loss of active Na ions consumed for the formation of a solid electrolyte interface.  To make up for the lost Na ion, presodiation treatments have been applied, which are effective ways to mitigate the low initial efficiency.  Here, we developed a direct-contact method to achieve the presodiation for cathode and anode electrodes and demonstrated the enhanced Coulombic efficiency of the 1st cycle with improved cyclability and reversible capacity.  We proved the formation of a thick passivation layer at the cathode-electrolyte interface during the presodiation process; this contributes to the improved cycle stability by preventing the dissoln. of the active material and its deposition on the anode surface.  The direct-contact method is a simple and cost-effective way to complete presodiation, and this simple process will be widely applicable for practical battery manufg.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2005.02.137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">EIS study on the formation of solid electrolyte interface in Li-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, S. S.; Xu, K.; Jow, T. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Formation of a solid/electrolyte interface (SEI) on the surface of graphite in Li/graphite batteries was studied by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS).  EIS of the cells vary considerably with cell voltage.  In particular, resistance, Rsei of the SEI increases with lithiation and decreases reversibly during de-lithiation in the voltage range 0.15 to 0.04 V.  According to the change of Rsei during the 1st lithiation, the formation of the SEI occurs above and below 0.15 V.  The SEI formed in the above 0.15 V is less conductive, and that formed below 0.15 V is highly conductive.  Ionic cond. of the preliminarily formed SEI is affected by the solvents and salts of the electrolyte.  The Rsei increases in the order LiBF4 &gt; LiSO3CF3 &gt; LiBOB &gt; LiPF6 for salt, and NMP &gt; EMC &gt; MB for solvent.  Addn. of vinylene carbonate to the electrolyte increases the Rsei.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1149/1.2128939"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, C.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The compositional variations of the thermodn. and mass transport properties of the β phase "LiAl" in the system were investigated at 415° to 600°. At the ideal stoichiometry, the std. Gibbs free energy of formation of "LiAl" is -29.2 kJ/mol at 415° and the corresponding enthalpy and entropy are -43.3 and -20.6 J/mol K, resp.  Two different electrochem. transient techniques were used to measure the chem. diffusion coeff. in "LiAl" as a function of the stoichiometry; the exptl. results obtained are in good agreement.  On the Li deficient side of ideal stoichiometry, the chem. diffusion coeff. increases with decreasing Li concn., becoming about 10-4 cm2/s near the phase boundary.  On the other hand, it is compn. independent on the Li excess side of "LiAl," varying with temp. from 2.4 × 10-6 cm2/s at 415° to 1.8 × 10-5 cm2/s at 600°.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/0013-4686(95)00386-X"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pyun, S.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsaelm.3c01091"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakajima, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ueno, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nagashio, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Work Function Modulation of Bi/Au Bilayer System toward p-Type WSe<sub>2</sub> FET</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                 10.1021/acsaelm.3c01091</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2023%26pages%3D144-149%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BElectron.%2BMater.%26author%3DR.%2BNakajima%26author%3DT.%2BNishimura%26author%3DK.%2BUeno%26author%3DK.%2BNagashio%26title%3DWork%2BFunction%2BModulation%2Bof%2BBi%252FAu%2BBilayer%2BSystem%2Btoward%2Bp-Type%2BWSe2%2BFET%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsaelm.3c01091&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c03971&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsaelm.3c01091&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1088/0957-4484/27/33/335603"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Doh, W. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeong, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J. Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Work function engineering of SnO single crystal microplates with thermal annealing</span>. <i>Nanotechnology</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>27</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">335603</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">335610</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1088/0957-4484/27/33/335603</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D27%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D335603-335610%26journal%3DNanotechnology%26author%3DW.%2BH.%2BDoh%26author%3DW.%2BJeong%26author%3DH.%2BLee%26author%3DJ.%2BPark%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BPark%26title%3DWork%2Bfunction%2Bengineering%2Bof%2BSnO%2Bsingle%2Bcrystal%2Bmicroplates%2Bwith%2Bthermal%2Bannealing%26doi%3D10.1088%252F0957-4484%252F27%252F33%252F335603&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c03971&amp;doiOfLink=10.1088%2F0957-4484%2F27%2F33%2F335603&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Work function engineering of SnO single crystal microplates with thermal annealing</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Doh, Won Hui; Jeong, Wooseok; Lee, Hyunsoo; Park, Jonghyurk; Park, Jeong Young</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">335603/1-335603/7</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNOTER</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1361-6528</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We synthesized black SnO single-crystal microplates via a sonochem. process and engineered the work function of the SnO microplates using thermal treatments.  The as-synthesized SnO microplates have a wide (001) plane, as is clearly evident from TEM images and diffraction patterns.  Surface potential measurements on the SnO microplates show that the work function changes as the annealing temp. increases.  The TEM and XAS results after thermal treatments imply that the micro-sized SnO(001) single-crystals are stable up to about 400°C in air, after which the surface starts to become locally oxidized.  Consequently, the long-range ordering and lattice parameter of the SnO(001) single crystals started to change to make polycryst. SnO2 at about 600°C.  These results demonstrate the ability to tune the work function of the microplates and suggest an intriguing way to engineer the elec. properties of nanostructures.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2020.06.072"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baddorf, A. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rondinone, A. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hensley, D. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Work function measurements of clean and modified carbon nanospikes</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>168</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">302</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">307</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2020.06.072</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D168%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D302-307%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DA.%2BP.%2BBaddorf%26author%3DA.%2BJ.%2BRondinone%26author%3DD.%2BK.%2BHensley%26title%3DWork%2Bfunction%2Bmeasurements%2Bof%2Bclean%2Band%2Bmodified%2Bcarbon%2Bnanospikes%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2020.06.072&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c03971&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2020.06.072&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188421c81154b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rapid growth in the market for elec. vehicles is imperative, to meet global targets for reducing greenhouse gas emissions, to improve air quality in urban centers and to meet the needs of consumers, with whom elec. vehicles are increasingly popular.  However, growing nos. of elec. vehicles present a serious waste-management challenge for recyclers at end-of-life.  Nevertheless, spent batteries may also present an opportunity as manufacturers require access to strategic elements and crit. materials for key components in elec.-vehicle manuf.: recycled Li-ion batteries from elec. vehicles could provide a valuable secondary source of materials.  Here the authors outline and evaluate the current range of approaches to elec.-vehicle Li-ion battery recycling and re-use, and highlight areas for future progress.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201900161"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abd El-Hady, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alshitari, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Al-Bogami, A.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Performance and cost of materials for lithium-based rechargeable automotive batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schmuch, Richard; Wagner, Ralf; Hoerpel, Gerhard; Placke, Tobias; Winter, Martin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">It is widely accepted that for elec. vehicles to be accepted by consumers and to achieve wide market penetration, ranges of at least 500 km at an affordable cost are required.  Therefore, significant improvements to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in terms of energy d. and cost along the battery value chain are required, while other key performance indicators, such as lifetime, safety, fast-charging ability and low-temp. performance, need to be enhanced or at least sustained.  Here, we review advances and challenges in LIB materials for automotive applications, in particular with respect to cost and performance parameters.  The prodn. processes of anode and cathode materials are discussed, focusing on material abundance and cost.  Advantages and challenges of different types of electrolyte for automotive batteries are examd.  Finally, energy densities and costs of promising battery chemistries are critically evaluated along with an assessment of the potential to fulfil the ambitious targets of elec. vehicle propulsion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1038/s41560-023-01374-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sustainable battery manufacturing in the future</span>. <i>Nat.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy storage will be key to overcoming the intermittency and variability of renewable energy sources.  Here, we propose a metric for the cost of energy storage and for identifying optimally sized storage systems.  The levelized cost of energy storage is the minimum price per kWh that a potential investor requires in order to break even over the entire lifetime of the storage facility.  We forecast the dynamics of this cost metric in the context of lithium-ion batteries and demonstrate its usefulness in identifying an optimally sized battery charged by an incumbent solar PV system.  Applying the model to residential solar customers in Germany, we find that behind-the-meter storage is economically viable because of the large difference between retail rates and current feed-in tariffs.  In contrast, investment incentives for battery systems in California derive principally from a state-level subsidy program.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium and Sodium-Ion Batteries: 50 Years of Research</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the rapid expansion in energy demands and depletion of fossil fuel reservoirs, the importance of clean energy prodn. and storage has increased drastically.  The renewable energy recourses are cost effective, sustainable and carbon dioxide emission free alternatives.  Nevertheless, this energy is not always available and needs to be stored.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are rapidly used in various applications such as powering electronics, elec. vehicles and grid energy storage.  However, the increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy and rise in cost of LIBs due to limited availability of lithium reserves arises doubts whether LIBs alone can meet the rising demands for mid-to-large-scale energy storage.  Therefore, attention has been shifted towards development of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) which have wide reserves and low precursor cost and thus is considered as appropriate choice for solar and wind energy development.  The prime problem encountered in development of large-scale SIBs is the low effectiveness of appropriate anode material because of large size and sluggish kinetics of Na ions.  A comprehensive study regarding anode materials is reported focusing on storage mechanism and structural changes involved during storage of Na ions in various classes of anode materials including carbon-based materials, conversion, conversion/alloying and org. materials.  A brief overview of various components of SIBs such as cathode, electrolyte and separator are also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.3c02892"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as promising energy storage devices owing to the similar "rocking chair" working mechanism as lithium-ion batteries and abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  However, the large ionic radius of the Na-ion (1.07 Å) brings a key scientific challenge, restricting the development of electrode materials for SIBs, and the infeasibility of graphite and silicon in reversible Na-ion storage further promotes the investigation of advanced anode materials.  Currently, the key issues facing anode materials include sluggish electrochem. kinetics and a large vol. expansion. Various optimization strategies to improve the electrochem. properties of anodes are summarized, including phase state adjustment, defect introduction, mol. engineering, nanostructure design, composite construction, heterostructure synthesis, and heteroatom doping.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have systematically changed the no. of at. layers stacked in 2D SnO nanosheet anodes and studied their sodium ion battery (SIB) performance.  The results indicate that as the no. of at. SnO layers in a sheet decreases, both the capacity and cycling stability of the Na ion battery improve.  The thinnest SnO nanosheet anodes (two to six SnO monolayers) exhibited the best performance.  Specifically, an initial discharge and charge capacity of 1072 and 848 mAh g-1 were obsd., resp., at 0.1 A g-1.  In addn., an impressive reversible capacity of 665 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 and 452 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles at a high c.d. of 1.0 A g-1 was obsd., with excellent rate performance.  As the av. no. of at. layers in the anode sheets increased, the battery performance degraded significantly.  For example, for the anode sheets with 10-20 at. layers, only a reversible capacity of 389 mAh g-1 could be obtained after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.  D. functional theory calcns. coupled with exptl. results were used to elucidate the sodiation mechanism of the SnO nanosheets.  This systematic study of monolayer-dependent phys. and electrochem. properties of 2D anodes shows a promising pathway to engineering and mitigating vol. changes in 2D anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  It also demonstrates that ultrathin SnO nanosheets are promising SIB anode materials with high specific capacity, stable cyclability, and excellent rate performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1016/S0378-7753(00)00450-X"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is challenging to develop alloying anodes with ultrafast charging and large energy storage using bulk anode materials because of the difficulty of carrier-ion diffusion and fragmentation of the active electrode material.  Herein, a rational strategy is reported to design bulk Bi anodes for Na-ion batteries that feature ultrafast charging, long cyclability, and large energy storage without using expensive nanomaterials and surface modifications.  It is found that bulk Bi particles gradually transform into a porous nanostructure during cycling in a glyme-based electrolyte, whereas the resultant structure stores Na ions by forming phases with high Na diffusivity.  These features allow the anodes to exhibit unprecedented electrochem. properties; the developed Na-Bi half-cell delivers 379 mA h g-1 (97% of that measured at 1C) at 7.7 A g-1 (20C) during 3500 cycles.  It also retained 94% and 93% of the capacity measured at 1C even at extremely fast-charging rates of 80C and 100C, resp.  The structural origins of the measured properties are verified by expts. and first-principles calcns.                 10.1016/j.electacta.2005.02.137</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">EIS study on the formation of solid electrolyte interface in Li-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, S. S.; Xu, K.; Jow, T. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2006</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">51</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8-9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1636-1640</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Formation of a solid/electrolyte interface (SEI) on the surface of graphite in Li/graphite batteries was studied by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS).  EIS of the cells vary considerably with cell voltage.  In particular, resistance, Rsei of the SEI increases with lithiation and decreases reversibly during de-lithiation in the voltage range 0.15 to 0.04 V.  According to the change of Rsei during the 1st lithiation, the formation of the SEI occurs above and below 0.15 V.  The SEI formed in the above 0.15 V is less conductive, and that formed below 0.15 V is highly conductive.  Ionic cond. of the preliminarily formed SEI is affected by the solvents and salts of the electrolyte.  The Rsei increases in the order LiBF4 &gt; LiSO3CF3 &gt; LiBOB &gt; LiPF6 for salt, and NMP &gt; EMC &gt; MB for solvent.  Addn. of vinylene carbonate to the electrolyte increases the Rsei.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1149/1.2128939"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, C. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boukamp, B. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huggins, R. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weppner, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Thermodynamic and mass transport properties of “Li-Al”</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1979</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>126</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2258</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2266</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.2128939</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermodynamic and mass transport properties of "lithium aluminide"</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, C. John; Boukamp, B. A.; Huggins, R. A.; Weppner, W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1979</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">126</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2258-66</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The compositional variations of the thermodn. and mass transport properties of the β phase "LiAl" in the system were investigated at 415° to 600°.  At 415° the emf. of the single phase "LiAl" lies between 300 and 70 mV relative to pure Li and this corresponds to a Li activity increasing from 0.0063 to 0.31 over the phase stability range from 46.8 to 55.0 at. % Li.  At the ideal stoichiometry, the std. Gibbs free energy of formation of "LiAl" is -29.2 kJ/mol at 415° and the corresponding enthalpy and entropy are -43.3 and -20.6 J/mol K, resp.  Two different electrochem. transient techniques were used to measure the chem. diffusion coeff. in "LiAl" as a function of the stoichiometry; the exptl. results obtained are in good agreement.  On the Li deficient side of ideal stoichiometry, the chem. diffusion coeff. increases with decreasing Li concn., becoming about 10-4 cm2/s near the phase boundary.  On the other hand, it is compn. independent on the Li excess side of "LiAl," varying with temp. from 2.4 × 10-6 cm2/s at 415° to 1.8 × 10-5 cm2/s at 600°.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1016/0013-4686(95)00386-X"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pyun, S. I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bae, J. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The AC impedance study of electrochemical lithium intercalation into porous vanadium oxide electrode</span>. <i>Electrochem. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>41</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">919</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">925</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0013-4686(95)00386-X</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
